<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Fonio millet genome unlocks African orphan crop diversity for agriculture in a changing climate"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2020 11:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-09-08"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Sustainable food production in the context of climate change necessitates diversification of agriculture and a more efficient utilization of plant genetic resources. Fonio millet (Digitaria exilis) is an orphan African cereal crop with a great potential for dryland agriculture. Here, we establish high-quality genomic resources to facilitate fonio improvement through molecular breeding. These include a chromosome-scale reference assembly and deep re-sequencing of 183 cultivated and wild Digitaria accessions, enabling insights into genetic diversity, population structure, and domestication. Fonio diversity is shaped by climatic, geographic, and ethnolinguistic factors. Two genes associated with seed size and shattering showed signatures of selection. Most known domestication genes from other cereal models however have not experienced strong selection in fonio, providing direct targets to rapidly improve this crop for agriculture in hot and dry environments. Fonio millet is a fast growing orphan cereal crop with a great potential for dryland agriculture. Here, the authors report chromosome-scale reference genome assembly and population genomic resources to shed light on genetic diversity, population structure and domestication of fonio millet."/>

Université de Montpellier, Cirad, INRAE, Institut Agro, Montpellier, France" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" data-author-popup="auth-Sylvie-Vancoppenolle">Sylvie Vancoppenolle</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff11">11</a>,<a href="#Aff12">12</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope"             <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-9896-1210"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-9896-1210</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a>,<a href="#Aff10">10</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="IRD" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.4399.7, 0000000122879528, DIADE, Univ Montpellier, IRD,  Montpellier, France" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Laboratoire Mixte International LAPSE" /><meta itemprop="address" content="Laboratoire Mixte International LAPSE, Dakar, Senegal" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Simon_G_-Krattinger" data-author-popup="auth-Simon_G_-Krattinger" data-corresp-id="c2">Simon G.             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Sustainable food production in the context of climate change necessitates diversification of agriculture and a more efficient utilization of plant genetic resources. Fonio millet (<i>Digitaria exilis</i>) is an orphan African cereal crop with a great potential for dryland agriculture. Here, we establish high-quality genomic resources to facilitate fonio improvement through molecular breeding. These include a chromosome-scale reference assembly and deep re-sequencing of 183 cultivated and wild <i>Digitaria</i> accessions, enabling insights into genetic diversity, population structure, and domestication. Fonio diversity is shaped by climatic, geographic, and ethnolinguistic factors. Two genes associated with seed size and shattering showed signatures of selection. Most known domestication genes from other cereal models however have not experienced strong selection in fonio, providing direct targets to rapidly improve this crop for agriculture in hot and dry environments.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Humanity faces the unprecedented challenge of having to sustainably produce healthy food for 9–10 billion people by 2050 in a context of climate change. A more efficient use of plant diversity and genetic resources in breeding has been recognized as a key priority to diversify and transform agriculture<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hickey, L. T. et al. Breeding crops to feed 10 billion. Nat. Biotechnol. 37, 744–754 (2019)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d56235e884">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tena, G. Sequencing forgotten crops. Nat. Plants 5, 5 (2019)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d56235e884_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="National Academies of Sciences Engineering and Medicine. Breakthroughs to Advance Food and Agricultural Research by 2030 (The National Academies Press, Washington, 2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d56235e887">3</a></sup>. The Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations (FAO) stated that arid and semi-arid regions are the most vulnerable environments to increasing uncertainties in regional and global food production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="FAO. The State of Agricultural Commodity Markets 2018. Agricultural Trade, Climate Change and Food Security (FAO, Rome, 2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d56235e891">4</a></sup>. In most countries of Africa and the Middle East, agricultural productivity will decline in the near future<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="FAO. The State of Agricultural Commodity Markets 2018. Agricultural Trade, Climate Change and Food Security (FAO, Rome, 2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d56235e895">4</a></sup>, because of climate change, land degradation, and groundwater depletion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Dalin, C., Wada, Y., Kastner, T. &amp; Puma, M. J. Groundwater depletion embedded in international food trade. Nature 543, 700–704 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d56235e899">5</a></sup>. Agricultural selection, from the early steps of domestication to modern-day crop breeding, has resulted in a marked decrease in agrobiodiversity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Fernie, A. R. &amp; Yan, J. De novo domestication: an alternative route toward new crops for the future. Mol. Plant 12, 615–631 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d56235e903">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Tanksley, S. D. &amp; McCouch, S. R. Seed banks and molecular maps: unlocking genetic potential from the wild. Science 277, 1063–1066 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d56235e906">7</a></sup>. Today, three cereal crops alone, bread wheat (<i>Triticum aestivum</i>), maize (<i>Zea mays</i>), and rice (<i>Oryza sativa</i>) account for more than half of the globally consumed calories<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Gruber, K. Agrobiodiversity: the living library. Nature 544, S8–S10 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d56235e920">8</a></sup>.</p><p>Many of today’s major cereal crops, including rice and maize, originated in relatively humid tropical and sub-tropical regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Kistler, L. et al. Multiproxy evidence highlights a complex evolutionary legacy of maize in South America. Science 362, 1309–1313 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d56235e927">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Wing, R. A., Purugganan, M. D. &amp; Zhang, Q. F. The rice genome revolution: from an ancient grain to Green Super Rice. Nat. Rev. Genet. 19, 505–517 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d56235e930">10</a></sup>. Although plant breeding has adapted the major cereal crops to a wide range of climates and cultivation practices, there is limited genetic diversity within these few plant species for cultivation in the most extreme environments. On the other hand, crop wild relatives and orphan crops are often adapted to extreme environments and their utility to unlock marginal lands for agriculture has recently regained interest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Tena, G. Sequencing forgotten crops. Nat. Plants 5, 5 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d56235e934">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Fernie, A. R. &amp; Yan, J. De novo domestication: an alternative route toward new crops for the future. Mol. Plant 12, 615–631 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d56235e937">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Eshed, Y. &amp; Lippman, Z. B. Revolutions in agriculture chart a course for targeted breeding of old and new crops. Science 366, eaax0025 (2019)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d56235e940">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kantar, M. B. &amp; Runck, B. Take a walk on the wild side. Nat. Clim. Change 9, 731–732 (2019)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d56235e940_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dawson, I. K. et al. The role of genetics in mainstreaming the production of new and orphan crops to diversify food systems and support human nutrition. New Phytol. 224, 37–54 (2019)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d56235e940_2">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Pironon, S. et al. Potential adaptive strategies for 29 sub-Saharan crops under future climate change. Nat. Clim. Change 9, 758–763 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d56235e943">14</a></sup>. Current technological advances in genomics and genome editing provide an opportunity to rapidly domesticate wild relatives and to improve orphan crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wallace, J. G., Rodgers-Melnick, E. &amp; Buckler, E. S. On the road to breeding 4.0: unraveling the good, the bad, and the boring of crop quantitative genomics. Annu. Rev. Genet. 52, 421–444 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d56235e947">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Chen, K., Wang, Y., Zhang, R., Zhang, H. &amp; Gao, C. CRISPR/Cas genome editing and precision plant breeding in agriculture. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 70, 667–697 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d56235e950">16</a></sup>. De novo domestication of wild species or rapid improvement of semi-domesticated crops can be achieved in less than a decade by targeting a few key genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Fernie, A. R. &amp; Yan, J. De novo domestication: an alternative route toward new crops for the future. Mol. Plant 12, 615–631 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d56235e954">6</a></sup>.</p><p>White fonio (<i>Digitaria exilis</i> (Kippist) Stapf) (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig1">1</a>) is an indigenous West African millet species with a great potential for agriculture in marginal environments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Barnaud, A. et al. High selfing rate inferred for white fonio [Digitaria exilis (Kippist.) Stapf] reproductive system opens up opportunities for breeding programs. Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 64, 1485–1490 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d56235e967">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Ayenan, M. A. T., Sodedji, K. A. F., Nwankwo, C. I., Olodo, K. F. &amp; Alladassi, M. E. B. Harnessing genetic resources and progress in plant genomics for fonio (Digitaria spp.) improvement. Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 65, 373–386 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d56235e970">18</a></sup>. Fonio is a small annual herbaceous C4 plant, which produces very small (<span class="stix">∼</span>1 mm) grains that are tightly surrounded by a husk<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Cruz, J. F. &amp; Beavogui, F. Fonio, an African Cereal (CIRAD, France, 2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d56235e974">19</a></sup> (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig1">1</a>). Fonio is cultivated under a large range of environmental conditions, from a tropical monsoon climate in western Guinea to a hot, arid desert climate in the Sahel zone. Some extra-early maturing fonio varieties produce mature grains in only 70–90 days<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Cruz, J. F. &amp; Beavogui, F. Fonio, an African Cereal (CIRAD, France, 2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d56235e982">19</a></sup>, which makes fonio one of the fastest maturing cereals. Because of its quick maturation, fonio is often grown to avoid food shortage during the lean season (period before main harvest), which is why fonio is also referred to as ‘hungry rice’. In addition, fonio is drought tolerant and adapted to nutrient-poor, sandy soils<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Adoukonou-Sagbadja, H., Wagner, C., Ordon, F. &amp; Friedt, W. Reproductive system and molecular phylogenetic relationships of fonio millets (Digitaria spp., Poaceae) with some polyploid wild relatives. Trop. Plant Biol. 3, 240–251 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d56235e986">20</a></sup>. Despite its local importance for agriculture in West Africa, fonio shows many unfavorable characteristics that resemble undomesticated plants: residual seed shattering, lodging, and lower yields than other cereals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Ayenan, M. A. T., Sodedji, K. A. F., Nwankwo, C. I., Olodo, K. F. &amp; Alladassi, M. E. B. Harnessing genetic resources and progress in plant genomics for fonio (Digitaria spp.) improvement. Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 65, 373–386 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d56235e990">18</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">1</a>). The most likely wild progenitor of cultivated fonio is the tetraploid annual weed <i>D. longiflora</i> that is widely distributed in tropical Africa. It has been suggested that fonio was domesticated more than 5000 years ago in the Inner Niger Delta of central Mali. Compared to <i>D. exilis</i>, the wild <i>D. longiflora</i> shows even heavier seed shattering and hairy spikelets, which suggests that some selection for reduced seed shattering and spikelet hairiness occurred in fonio<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Abdul, S. D. &amp; Jideani, A. I. O. Fonio (Digitaria spp.) breeding. In Advances in Plant Breeding Strategies: Cereals (eds Al-Khayri, J. M., Jain, S. M. &amp; Johnson, D. V.) 47–81 (Springer, 2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1007">21</a></sup>. In the past few years, fonio has gained in popularity inside and outside of West Africa because of its nutritional qualities.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Phenotype of fonio (Digitaria exilis)."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Phenotype of fonio (<i>Digitaria exilis</i>).</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18329-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18329_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18329-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18329_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="677"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Field of cultivated fonio in Guinea. <b>b</b> Grains of maize, wheat, rice, and fonio (from left to right). <b>c</b> Plants of the fonio accession CM05836.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Here, we present the establishment of a comprehensive set of genomic resources for fonio, which constitutes the first step towards harnessing the potential of this cereal crop for agriculture in harsh environments. These resources include the generation of a high-quality, chromosome-scale reference assembly and the deep re-sequencing of a diversity panel that includes wild and cultivated accessions covering a wide geographic range.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Chromosome-scale fonio reference genome assembly</h3><p>Fonio is a tetraploid species (2<i>n</i> = 4× = 36)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Adoukonou-Sagbadja, H. et al. Flow cytometric analysis reveals different nuclear DNA contents in cultivated Fonio (Digitaria spp.) and some wild relatives from West-Africa. Plant Syst. Evol. 267, 163–176 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1061">22</a></sup> with a highly inbreeding reproductive system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Barnaud, A. et al. High selfing rate inferred for white fonio [Digitaria exilis (Kippist.) Stapf] reproductive system opens up opportunities for breeding programs. Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 64, 1485–1490 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1065">17</a></sup>. To build a <i>D. exilis</i> reference assembly, we chose an accession from one of the driest regions of fonio cultivation, CM05836 from the Mopti region in Mali. The size of the CM05836 genome was estimated to be 893 Mb/1C by flow cytometry (Supplementary Figs.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">3</a>), which is in line with previous reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Adoukonou-Sagbadja, H. et al. Flow cytometric analysis reveals different nuclear DNA contents in cultivated Fonio (Digitaria spp.) and some wild relatives from West-Africa. Plant Syst. Evol. 267, 163–176 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1079">22</a></sup>. The CM05836 genome was sequenced and assembled using deep sequencing of multiple short-read libraries (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">1</a>), including Illumina paired-end (321-fold coverage), mate-pair (241-fold coverage) and linked-read (10× Genomics, 84-fold coverage) sequencing. The raw reads were assembled and scaffolded with the software package DeNovoMAGIC3 (NRGene), which has recently been used to assemble various high-quality plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93–97 (2017)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1086">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Origin and evolution of the octoploid strawberry genome. Nat. Genet. 51, 541–547 (2019)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1086_1">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Springer, N. M. et al. The maize W22 genome provides a foundation for functional genomics and transposon biology. Nat. Genet. 50, 1282–1288 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1089">25</a></sup>. Integration of Hi-C reads (122-fold coverage, Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">2</a>) and a Bionano optical map (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">3</a>) resulted in a chromosome-scale assembly with a total length of 716,471,022 bp, of which ~91.5% (655,723,161 bp) were assembled in 18 pseudomolecules. A total of 60.75 Mb were unanchored (Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Tab1">1</a>). Of 1440 Embryophyta single copy core genes (BUSCO v3.0.2), 96.1% were recovered in the CM05836 assembly, 2.9% were missing, and 1% was fragmented. As no genetic <i>D. exilis</i> map is available, we used chromosome painting to further assess the quality of the CM05836 assembly. Pools of short oligonucleotides covering each one of the 18 pseudomolecules were designed based on the CM05836 assembly, fluorescently labeled, and hybridized to mitotic metaphase chromosome spreads of CM05836<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Han, Y. H., Zhang, T., Thammapichai, P., Weng, Y. Q. &amp; Jiang, J. M. Chromosome-specific painting in Cucumis species using bulked oligonucleotides. Genetics 200, 771–779 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1106">26</a></sup>. Each of the 18 libraries specifically hybridized to only one chromosome pair, confirming that our assembly unambiguously distinguished homoeologous chromosomes (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig2">2a</a>, Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">4</a>). Centromeric regions contained a tandem repeat with a 314 bp long unit, which was found in all fonio chromosomes (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">5</a>). We also re-assembled all the data with the open-source TRITEX pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Monat, C. et al. TRITEX: chromosome-scale sequence assembly of Triticeae genomes with open-source tools. Genome Biol. 20, 284 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1120">27</a></sup> and the two assemblies showed a high degree of collinearity (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of the fonio genome assembly and annotation.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fonio genome features."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Fonio genome features.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18329-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18329_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18329-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18329_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="665"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Representative example of oligo painting FISH on mitotic metaphase chromosomes. Shown are probes designed from pseudomolecules 9A (green) and 9B (red) of the CM05836 assembly (scale bar = 5 µm). Oligo painting FISH experiment was repeated independently three times. <b>b</b> Principal component analysis (PCA) of the transposable element cluster RLG_Loris (upper panel) that allowed discrimination of the two sub-genomes. Blue dots represent elements found on the A sub-genome; pink triangles represent elements from the B sub-genome; black squares represent elements present on chromosome unanchored. PCA of the transposable element cluster RLG_Elodie (lower panel) that was specific to the B sub-genome. <b>c</b> Synteny and distribution of genome features. (I) Number and length of the pseudomolecules. The gray and black colors represent the two sub-genomes. (II, III) Density of genes and repeats along each pseudomolecule, respectively. Lines in the inner circle represent the homoeologous relationships. <b>d</b> Maximum likehood tree of 11 <i>Poaceae</i> species based on 30 orthologous gene groups. Topologies are supported by 1000 bootstrap replicates. Colors indicate the different clades.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We compared the fonio pseudomolecule structure to foxtail millet (<i>Setaria italica</i>; 2<i>n</i> = 2× = 18), a diploid relative with a fully sequenced genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Bennetzen, J. L. et al. Reference genome sequence of the model plant Setaria. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 555–561 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1489">28</a></sup>. The fonio genome shows a syntenic relationship with the genome of foxtail millet, with two homoeologous sets of nine fonio chromosomes (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">7</a>). Without a clear diploid ancestor, we could not directly disentangle the two sub-genomes based on genomic information from diploid ancestors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Tang, H. Disentangling a polyploid genome. Nat. Plants 3, 688–689 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1496">29</a></sup>. We thus used a genetic structure approach based on full-length long terminal repeat retrotransposons (fl-LTR-RT) as an alternative strategy. A total of 11 fl-LTR-RT families with more than 30 elements were identified and defined as a ‘populations’, allowing us to apply genetic structure analyses that are often used in population genomics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Suguiyama, V. F., Vasconcelos, L. A. B., Rossi, M. M., Biondo, C. &amp; de Setta, N. The population genetic structure approach adds new insights into the evolution of plant LTR retrotransposon lineages. PLoS ONE 14, e0214542 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1501">30</a></sup>. We searched for fl-LTR-RT clusters that only appeared in one of the two homoeologous sub-genomes. Out of the 11 fl-LTR-RT populations analyzed, two allowed us to discriminate the sub-genomes (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig2">2b</a>, Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">8</a>). The two families belong to the Gypsy superfamily and dating of insertion time was estimated to be ~1.56 million years ago (MYA) (58 elements, 0.06–4.67 MYA) and ~1.14 MYA (36 elements, 0.39–1.96 MYA), respectively. The two putative sub-genomes were designated A and B and chromosome numbers were assigned based on the synteny with foxtail millet (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">7</a>).</p><p>Gene annotation was performed using the MAKER pipeline (v3.01.02) with 34.1% of the fonio genome masked as repetitive. Transcript sequences of CM05836 from flag leaves, grains, panicles, and whole above-ground seedlings (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">4</a>) in combination with protein sequences of publicly available plant genomes were used to annotate the CM05836 assembly. This resulted in the annotation of 59,844 protein-coding genes (57,023 on 18 pseudomolecules and 2821 on unanchored chromosome) with an average length of 2.5 kb and an average exon number of 4.6. The analysis of the four CM05836 RNA-seq samples showed that 44,542 protein coding genes (74.3%) were expressed (&gt;0.5 transcripts per million), which is comparable to the annotation of the bread wheat genome (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">5</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding through a fully annotated and anchored reference genome sequence. Science 361, eaar7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1523">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Ramirez-Gonzalez, R. H. et al. The transcriptional landscape of polyploid wheat. Science 361, eaar6089 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1526">32</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Synteny with other cereals</h3><p>Whole genome comparative analyses of the CM05836 genome with other grass species were consistent with the previously established phylogenetic relationships of fonio<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Adoukonou-Sagbadja, H., Wagner, C., Ordon, F. &amp; Friedt, W. Reproductive system and molecular phylogenetic relationships of fonio millets (Digitaria spp., Poaceae) with some polyploid wild relatives. Trop. Plant Biol. 3, 240–251 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1538">20</a></sup>. A comparison of the CM05836 A and B sub-genomes identified a set of 16,514 homoeologous gene pairs that fulfilled the criteria for evolutionary analyses (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig2">2c</a> and Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM5">1</a>). The estimation of synonymous substitution rates (Ks) among homoeologous gene pairs revealed a divergence time of the two sub-genomes of roughly 3 MYA (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">9</a> and Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM5">1</a>). These results indicate that <i>D. exilis</i> is a recent allotetraploid species. A Ks distribution using orthologous genes revealed that fonio diverged from the other members of the Paniceae tribe (broomcorn millet (<i>Panicum miliaceum</i>), Hall’s panicgrass (<i>P. hallii</i>), and foxtail millet (<i>S. italica</i>)) between 14.6 and 16.9 MYA, and from the Andropogoneae tribe (sorghum (<i>Sorghum bicolor</i>) and maize (<i>Z. mays</i>)) between 21.5 and 26.9 MYA. Bread wheat (<i>T. aestivum</i>), goatgrass (<i>Aegilops tauschii</i>), barley (<i>Hordeum vulgare</i>), rice (<i>O. sativa</i>), and purple false brome (<i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>) showed a divergence time of 35.3–40 MYA (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig2">2d</a>). The phylogenetic position of <i>D. exilis</i> as a basal taxon of the Paniceae allowed us to reconstruct the hypothetical ancestral genomic state of this tribe (Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM6">2</a>).</p><p>The hybridization of two genomes can result in genome instability, extensive gene loss, and reshuffling. As a consequence, one sub-genome may evolve dominancy over the other sub-genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Origin and evolution of the octoploid strawberry genome. Nat. Genet. 51, 541–547 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1602">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding through a fully annotated and anchored reference genome sequence. Science 361, eaar7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1605">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bird, K. A., VanBuren, R., Puzey, J. R. &amp; Edger, P. P. The causes and consequences of subgenome dominance in hybrids and recent polyploids. New Phytol. 220, 87–93 (2018)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1608">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1608_1">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Shi, J. P. et al. Chromosome conformation capture resolved near complete genome assembly of broomcorn millet. Nat. Commun. 10, 464 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1611">35</a></sup>. Using foxtail millet as a reference, the fonio A and B sub-genomes showed similar numbers of orthologous genes: 14,235 and 14,153, respectively. Out of these, 12,541 were retained as duplicates while 1694 and 1612 were specific to the A and B sub-genomes, respectively. The absence of sub-genome dominance was also supported by similar gene expression levels between homoeologous pairs of genes (Mann–Whitney <i>U</i> test; <i>p</i> = 0.66; Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">10</a> and Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM7">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Evolutionary history of fonio and its wild relative</h3><p>To get an overview of the diversity and evolution of fonio, we selected 166 <i>D. exilis</i> accessions originating from Guinea, Mali, Benin, Togo, Burkina Faso, Ghana, and Niger and 17 accessions of the proposed wild tetraploid fonio progenitor <i>D. longiflora</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Abdul, S. D. &amp; Jideani, A. I. O. Fonio (Digitaria spp.) breeding. In Advances in Plant Breeding Strategies: Cereals (eds Al-Khayri, J. M., Jain, S. M. &amp; Johnson, D. V.) 47–81 (Springer, 2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1640">21</a></sup> from Cameroon, Nigeria, Guinea, Chad, Sudan, Kenya, Gabon, and Congo for whole-genome re-sequencing (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">6</a>). The selection was done from a collection of 641 georeferenced <i>D. exilis</i> accessions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Clément, J. &amp; Leblanc, J. M. Collecte IBPGR-ORSTOM de 1977 au Togo (Catalogue ORSTOM, 1984)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1650">36</a></sup> with the aim of maximizing diversity based on bioclimatic data and geographic origin. We obtained short-read sequences with an average of 45-fold coverage for <i>D. exili</i>s (range 36–61-fold) and 20-fold coverage for <i>D. longiflora</i> (range 10–28-fold) (Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM8">4</a>). The average mapping rates of the raw reads to the CM05836 reference assembly were 85% and 68% for <i>D. exilis</i> and <i>D. longiflora</i>, respectively, with most accessions showing a mapping rate of &gt;80% (Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM9">5</a>). After filtering, 11,046,501 high quality bi-allelic single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) were retained (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">7</a>). Nine <i>D. exilis</i> and three <i>D. longiflora</i> accessions were discarded based on the amount of missing data. The error rate of variant calling (proportion of segregating sites in a re-sequenced CM05836 sample compared to the reference assembly) was 0.04%, which is comparable to other studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Ramu, P. et al. Cassava haplotype map highlights fixation of deleterious mutations during clonal propagation. Nat. Genet. 49, 959–963 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1683">37</a></sup>. The re-sequenced CM05836 sample showed the lowest genetic divergence from the reference assembly of all re-sequenced accessions (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">11a</a>). The SNPs were evenly distributed across the 18 <i>D. exilis</i> chromosomes, with a tendency toward a lower SNP density at the chromosome ends (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">11b</a>). Approximately 30.2% of the SNPs were gene-proximal (2 kb upstream or downstream of a coding sequence), 9.5% in introns and 6.2% in exons. Of the exonic SNPs, 354,854 (51.6%) resulted in non-synonymous sequence changes, of which 6727 disrupted the coding sequence (premature stop codon). The remaining 333,296 (48.4%) exonic SNPs represented synonymous variants. Forty-four percent of the total SNPs (4,901,160 SNPs) were rare variants with a minor allele frequency of &lt;0.01 (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">12</a>). The vast majority of the rare variants was found in a few very diverse <i>D. longiflora</i> accessions. The mean nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) was 6.19 × 10<sup>−4</sup> and 3.68 × 10<sup>−3</sup> for <i>D. exilis</i> and <i>D. longiflora</i>, respectively. Genome-wide linkage disequilibrium (LD) analyses revealed a faster LD decay in <i>D. longiflora</i> (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup> ~ 0.16 at 70 kb) compared to <i>D. exilis</i> (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup> ~ 0.20 at 70 kb) (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">13</a>).</p><p>A PCA showed a clear genetic differentiation between cultivated <i>D. exilis</i> and wild <i>D. longiflora</i>. The <i>D. exilis</i> accessions clustered closely together, while the <i>D. longiflora</i> accessions split into three groups (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig3">3a</a>). The <i>D. longiflora</i> group that showed the greatest genetic distance from <i>D. exilis</i> contained three accessions originating from Central (Cameroon) and East Africa (South Sudan and Kenya). The geographical projection of the first principal component (which separated wild accessions from the cultivated accessions) revealed that the <i>D. exilis</i> accessions genetically closest to <i>D. longiflora</i> originated from southern Togo and the west of Guinea (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">14a, b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Genetic diversity and structure of D. exilis and D. longiflora diversity panel."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Genetic diversity and structure of <i>D. exilis</i> and <i>D. longiflora</i> diversity panel.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18329-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18329_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18329-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18329_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="612"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Principal component analysis (PCA) of 157 <i>D. exilis</i> and 14 <i>D. longiflora</i> accessions using whole-genome single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs). <i>D. exilis</i> samples (circles), <i>D. longiflora</i> (triangles). <b>b</b> PCA of <i>D. exilis</i> accessions alone. Colors indicate the country of origin. <b>c</b> Population structure (from <i>K</i> = 3 to <i>K</i> = 6) of <i>D. exilis</i> accessions estimated with sNMF. Each bar represents an accession and the bars are filled by colors representing the likelihood of membership to each ancestry. Accessions are ordered from west to east; Guinea (Gu), Mali (M), Burkina Faso (B.F), Ghana (Gh), Togo (T), Benin (B), and Niger (N). <b>d</b> Geographic distribution of ancestry proportions of <i>D. exilis</i> accessions obtained from the structure analysis at <i>K</i> = 6. The colors represent the maximal local contribution of an ancestry. Black dots represent the coordinates of <i>D. exilis</i> accessions. <b>e</b> Effective population size history of <i>D. exilis</i> groups based on <i>K</i> = 6 and <i>D. longiflora</i> (in black).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A PCA with <i>D. exilis</i> accessions alone revealed three main clusters. The second principal component separated eight accessions from southern Togo from the remaining accessions (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig3">3b</a>). In addition, five accessions from Guinea formed a distinct genetic group in the PCA. The remaining accessions were spread along the first axis of the PCA, mainly revealing a grouping by geographic location (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig3">3b</a>). The genetic clustering was confirmed by genetic structure analyses (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig3">3c, d</a>). The cross-validation error decreased with increasing <i>K</i> and reached a plateau starting from <i>K</i> = 6 (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">14c</a>). At <i>K</i> = 3, the eight South Togo accessions formed a distinct homogenous population. At <i>K</i> = 4, the five accessions from Guinea were separated. With increasing <i>K</i>, the admixture plot provided some evidence that natural (climate and geography) and human (ethnicity and language) factors had an effect on shaping the genetic population structure of fonio (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig3">3c, d</a>, Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">14d</a>). We observed a significant correlation (Pearson’s correlation; <i>p</i> &lt; 0.05, df = 155) between the genetic population structure (first principal component of PCA) and climate (i.e., mean temperature and precipitation of the wettest quarters) as well as geography (i.e., latitude, longitude, and altitude) (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig3">3b</a>, Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">15</a>, Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM10">6</a>). The relationship between genetic differentiation (genetic distance matrix) and climate was still significant when accounting for geographic distance (partial Mantel test; Mantel <i>r</i> = 0.30; <i>p</i> = 0.001). A significant correlation was also observed between the genetic distance matrix and the dissimilarity matrices of ethnic and linguistic groups (fisher test; <i>p</i> = 0.0005, Mantel test; Mantel <i>r</i> ethnic = −0.19; Mantel <i>r</i> linguistic = −0.13; <i>p</i> = 0.001). The effect of ethnic groups remained significant even when we controlled for geographic and climatic factors (ANCOVA; <i>p</i> = 0.045; df = 31) (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">8</a>). Association analyses based on climate variables revealed 38 and 179 loci that might be involved in adaptation to mean temperature and mean precipitation of the wettest quarters, respectively (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">16</a>, Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM11">7</a>). Loci associated with temperature contained genes enriched for functions related to hormone metabolic processes, hormone biosynthesis, carbohydrate metabolic processes, homeostatic process, plant development processes, and plant growth (shoot apical meristem maintenance, root growth and development) (Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM11">7</a>). Similarly, genes associated with precipitation were enriched in functions related to plant development and growth (Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM11">7</a>). A genome-wide association study with ethnic groups revealed significant associations for 55 and 227 SNPs with Bambara and Fula ethnic groups, respectively (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">17</a>, Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM11">7</a>). In particular, there were three prominent peaks on chromosomes 2A, 2B, and 6A. The peaks on chromosomes 2A and 2B fell into a region that contained a homolog of the Arabidopsis <i>WSD1</i> wax ester synthase/diacylglycerol acyltransferase gene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Patwari, P. et al. Surface wax esters contribute to drought tolerance in Arabidopsis. Plant J. 98, 727–744 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1956">38</a></sup>. <i>WSD1</i> is involved in accumulation of waxes under drought stress, possibly indicating selection for adaptation to drought by certain ethnic groups. Unadmixed populations were mainly found at the geographic extremes of the fonio cultivation area in the north and south, whereas the accessions from the central regions of West Africa tended to show a higher degree of admixture (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">18a</a>).</p><p>Plotting the spatial distribution of private SNPs (i.e., SNP present only once in a single accession) revealed a hotspot of rare alleles in Togo, Niger, the western part of Guinea, and southern Mali (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">18b, c</a>). Rare allele diversity was lower in the eastern part of Guinea, and in southwest Mali. Inference of the <i>D. exilis</i> effective population size revealed a decline that started more than 10,000 years ago and reached a minimum between 2000 and 1000 years ago (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig3">3e</a>). Then, a steep increase of the effective population size occurred to a level that was ~100-fold higher compared to the bottleneck.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genomic footprints of selection and domestication</h3><p>We used three complementary approaches to detect genomic regions under selection: (i) a composite likelihood-ratio (CLR) test based on site frequency spectrum (SFS)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Pavlidis, P., Zivkovic, D., Stamatakis, A. &amp; Alachiotis, N. SweeD: Likelihood-based detection of selective sweeps in thousands of genomes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2224–2234 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d56235e1986">39</a></sup>, (ii) the nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) ratios between <i>D. exilis</i> and <i>D. longiflora</i> over sliding genomic windows, and (iii) the genetic differentiation based on <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> calculations, again computed over sliding windows. With the CLR test, 78 regions were identified as candidates for signatures of selection. The genetic diversity ratios and <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> calculations revealed 311 and 208 regions, respectively (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig4">4</a>, Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM12">8</a>). We then searched for the presence of orthologs of known domestication genes in the regions under selection. The most striking candidate was one of the two orthologs of the rice grain size <i>GS5</i> gene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Li, Y. et al. Natural variation in GS5 plays an important role in regulating grain size and yield in rice. Nat. Genet. 43, 1266–1269 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2017">40</a></sup> (Dexi3A01G0012320 referred to as <i>DeGS5-3A</i>) that was detected by genetic diversity ratio and <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>-based calculations (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig4">4</a>). <i>GS5</i> regulates grain width and weight in rice. <i>D. exilis</i> showed a dramatic loss of genetic diversity at the <i>DeGS5-3A</i> gene (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig5">5a, b</a>). Domestication of fonio is associated with wider grains of <i>D. exilis</i> compared to grains of <i>D. longiflora</i> (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig5">5c</a>). The region of the <i>GS5</i> ortholog on chromosome 3B (<i>DeGS5-3B</i>) was not identified as being under selection and showed higher levels of nucleotide diversity than the <i>DeGS5-3A</i> region (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig5">5a, b</a>). Only the <i>DeGS5-3A</i> but not the <i>DeGS5-3B</i> transcript was detected in the <i>D. exilis</i> RNA-Seq data from grains. This is in agreement with observations made in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Li, Y. et al. Natural variation in GS5 plays an important role in regulating grain size and yield in rice. Nat. Genet. 43, 1266–1269 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2077">40</a></sup>, where a dominant mutation resulting in increased <i>GS5</i> transcript levels affects grain size.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Detection of selection in fonio."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Detection of selection in fonio.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18329-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18329_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18329-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18329_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="654"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Manhattan plots showing detection of selection along the genome based on nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) ratio, <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> and SweeD (from top to bottom). The location of orthologous genes of major seed shattering and plant architecture genes are indicated in the Manhattan plots. The black dashed lines indicate the 1% threshold. Some extreme outliers in the <i>π</i> ratio plot are not shown.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Selective sweep at the GS5 locus in fonio."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Selective sweep at the <i>GS5</i> locus in fonio.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18329-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18329_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18329-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18329_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="329"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> Smoothed representation of nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) in <i>D. exilis</i> in a 100 kb window surrounding the ortholog of the rice <i>GS5</i> gene. The green curve shows <i>π</i> around <i>DeGS5-3A</i> on chromosome 3A and the blue curve&nbsp;shows <i>π</i> around <i>DeGS5-3B</i> on chromosome 3B. The dashed vertical red lines represent the location of the <i>GS5</i> genes. The nucleotide diversity was calculated in overlapping 100 bp windows every 25 bp. <b>b</b> Schematic representation of the annotated genes in the <i>GS5</i> regions and the orthologous gene relationships between chromosomes 3A (green) and 3B (blue). <i>UF</i> protein of unknown function, <i>E3</i> E3 ubiquitin ligase, <i>SCP</i> (<i>GS5</i>) serine carboxypeptidase (Dexi3A01G0012320), <i>GAOX</i> gibberellin 2-beta-dioxygenase, <i>ECH</i> golgi apparatus membrane protein. <i>DeGS5-3A</i> and <i>DeGS5-3B</i> are indicated in red. The numbers in blue above and below the <i>GS5</i> orthologs show their respective expression in transcripts per million (TPM) in grain tissue. <b>c</b> Two grains of <i>D. exilis</i> (top) and <i>D. longiflora</i> (bottom).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Another domestication gene that was detected in the selection scan was an ortholog of the sorghum <i>Shattering 1</i> (<i>Sh1</i>) gene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Lin, Z. W. et al. Parallel domestication of the Shattering1 genes in cereals. Nat. Genet. 44, 720–724 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2218">41</a></sup>. <i>Sh1</i> encodes a YABBY transcription factor and the non-shattering phenotype in cultivated sorghum is associated with lower expression levels of <i>Sh1</i> (mutations in regulatory regions or introns) or truncated transcripts. Domesticated African rice (<i>O. glaberrima</i>) carries a 45 kb deletion at the orthologous <i>OsSh1</i> locus compared to its wild relative <i>O. barthii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Wang, M. H. et al. The genome sequence of African rice (Oryza glaberrima) and evidence for independent domestication. Nat. Genet. 46, 982–988 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2237">42</a></sup>. Around 37% of the fonio accessions had a 60 kb deletion similar to <i>O. glaberrima</i> that eliminated the <i>Sh1</i> ortholog on chromosome 9A (<i>DeSh1-9A</i>—Dexi9A01G0015055) (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig6">6a</a>). This deletion was identified by the lack of mapped reads and was confirmed by PCR amplification and Sanger sequencing. The homoeologous region including <i>DeSh1-9B</i> (Dexi9B01G0013485) on chromosome 9B was intact&nbsp;(Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig6">6b</a>). Interestingly, accessions with the <i>DeSh1-9A</i> deletion showed a minor (7%) but significant reduction in seed shattering (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#Fig6">6c</a>) compared to accessions with the intact <i>DeSh1-9A</i> gene (ANOVA; <i>p</i> = 0.008; df = 1). Accessions carrying this deletion were distributed across the whole range of fonio cultivation, which suggests that the deletion is ancient and might have been selected for in certain regions.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Selective sweep at the Sh1 locus in fonio."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Selective sweep at the <i>Sh1</i> locus in fonio.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18329-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18329_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18329-4/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18329_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="218"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b> Schematic representation of genes in the orthologous regions of the sorghum <i>Sh1</i> gene. The top most panel shows the region on fonio chromosome 9A with the 60 kb deletion as it is found in 37% of all <i>D. exilis</i> accessions. The lower panel shows the genes in accessions without the deletion. The <i>Sh1</i> ortholog <i>DeSh1-9A</i> is shown in red. <b>b</b> The <i>Sh1</i> ortholog <i>DeSh1-9B</i> on chromosome 9B is present in all <i>D. exilis</i> accessions. The dashed lines represent orthologous relationships between the two sub-genomes. <i>UF</i> protein of unknown function, <i>CDK</i> cyclin-dependent kinase, <i>Sh1</i> ortholog of sorghum <i>Shattering 1</i> (<i>Sh1</i>), <i>ST</i> sulfate transporter; <i>NT5C3B</i> 7-methylguanosine phosphate-specific 5’-nucleotidase; <i>MBD</i> methyl-CpG-binding domain. <b>c</b> The violin plots show the probability distribution of the seed shattering percentage in fonio accessions carrying the <i>DeSh1-9A</i> deletion (∆<i>DeSh1-9A</i>) in pink compared to accessions with an intact <i>DeSh1-9A</i> in blue. The shape of the distribution indicates that the seed shattering percentages for both groups are concentrated around the median. The center of each plot depicts a boxplot, the box represents the interquartile range (IQR), the middle horizontal line represents the median, the vertical lines going down and up from the box are defined as first quartile (−1.5 IQR) and third quartile (+1.5 IQR), respectively. Circles in the plot represent the seed shattering percentage calculated from individual fonio panicles, one circle per panicle, jitter option was added to show all individual observations that overlapped. Mean seed shattering was 45% in ∆<i>DeSh1-9A</i> (±0.18 s.d.) and 52% in <i>DeSh1-9A</i> (±0.22 s.d.). (<i>n</i> = Three individual panicles of 43 ∆<i>DeSh1-9A</i> accessions and 39 <i>DeSh-9A</i> accessions, respectively; two-way ANOVA <i>p</i> = 0.008; df = 1).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>Here, we established a set of genomic resources that allowed us to comprehensively assess the genetic variation found in fonio, a cereal crop that holds great promises for agriculture in marginal environments. The analysis of fl-LTR-RT revealed two sub-genome specific transposon clusters that experienced a peak of activity around 1.1–1.5 MYA, indicating that the two fonio sub-genomes hybridized after this period<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Exceptional subgenome stability and functional divergence in the allotetraploid Ethiopian cereal teff. Nat. Commun. 11, 884 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2390">43</a></sup>. The Ks analysis estimated that the two sub-genomes diverged prior to these transposon bursts—3 MYA, suggesting that fonio is an allotetraploid species.</p><p>The analysis of effective population size revealed a genetic bottleneck that was most likely associated with human cultivation and domestication. The large increase of effective size for fonio after a period of reduction was most probably due to the development and expansion of fonio cultivation. This expansion appears to be a recent event and occurred during the last millennium. The progression of the effective population size resembles the patterns observed for other domesticated crops, with a protracted period of cultivation followed by a marked bottleneck<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Cubry, P. et al. The rise and fall of African rice cultivation revealed by analysis of 246 new genomes. Curr. Biol. 28, 2274–2282 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2397">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Liang, Z. et al. Whole-genome resequencing of 472 Vitis accessions for grapevine diversity and demographic history analyses. Nat. Commun. 10, 1190 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2400">45</a></sup>. In the case of fonio, this potential bottleneck appears to be milder compared to other crops. It has been observed that the effective population size of the wild rice (<i>O. barthii</i>) from West Africa followed a trend similar to the cultivated African rice (<i>O. glaberrima</i>), which has been interpreted as a result of environmental degradation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Cubry, P. et al. The rise and fall of African rice cultivation revealed by analysis of 246 new genomes. Curr. Biol. 28, 2274–2282 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2410">44</a></sup> rather than human selection. In contrast, no signal of population bottleneck was observed for the proposed wild fonio ancestor <i>D. longiflora</i>, indicating that the bottleneck observed in fonio is associated with human cultivation. We also highlighted the strong impact of geographic, climatic and anthropogenic factors on shaping the genetic diversity of fonio. Even if fonio is not a dominant crop across West Africa, it benefits from cultural embedding and plays a key role in ritual systems in many African cultures<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Blench, R. M. Vernacular names for African millets and other minor cereals and their significance for agricultural history. Archaeol. Anthropol. Sci. 8, 1–8 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2418">46</a></sup>. For example, we observed a striking genetic differentiation between <i>D. exilis</i> accessions collected from northern and southern Togo. This can be attributed to both climatic and cultural differences. While the southern regions of Togo receive high annual rainfalls, the northern parts receive &lt;1000 mm annual rainfall and there is a prolonged dry season<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Adoukonou-Sagbadja, H., Dansi, A., Vodouhe, R. &amp; Akpagana, K. Collecting fonio (Digitaria exilis Kipp. Stapf, D. iburua Stapf) landraces in Togo. Plant Genet. Resour. Newsl. 139, 63–67 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2425">47</a></sup>.
