Adoukonou-Sagbadja et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Adoukonou-Sagbadja, H., Dansi, A., Vodouhe, R. &amp; Akpagana, K. Collecting fonio (Digitaria exilis Kipp. Stapf, D. iburua Stapf) landraces in Togo. Plant Genet. Resour. Newsl. 139, 63–67 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2429">47</a></sup> also noted that there is no seed exchange between farmers of the two regions because of cultural factors.</p><p>Despite a genetic bottleneck and the reduction in genetic diversity, fonio still shows many ‘wild’ characteristics such as residual seed shattering, lack of apical dominance, and lodging. We show that orthologs of most of the well-characterized domestication genes from other cereals were not under strong selection in fonio. Interestingly, an ortholog of the rice <i>GS5</i> gene (<i>DeGS5-3A</i>) was identified in a selective sweep. Dominant mutations in the <i>GS5</i> promoter region were associated with higher <i>GS5</i> transcript levels and wider and heavier grains in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Li, Y. et al. Natural variation in GS5 plays an important role in regulating grain size and yield in rice. Nat. Genet. 43, 1266–1269 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2448">40</a></sup>. The <i>DeGS5-3A</i> gene showed a complete loss of diversity in the coding sequence in <i>D. exilis</i>, suggesting a strong artificial selection for larger grains. In contrast, <i>DeSh1-9A</i> showed evidence for a partial selective sweep<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Meyer, R. S. &amp; Purugganan, M. D. Evolution of crop species: genetics of domestication and diversification. Nat. Rev. Genet. 14, 840–852 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2462">48</a></sup>. The non-shattering phenotype associated with the <i>Sh1</i> locus in sorghum is recessive<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Lin, Z. W. et al. Parallel domestication of the Shattering1 genes in cereals. Nat. Genet. 44, 720–724 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2469">41</a></sup> and the deletion of a single <i>DeSh1</i> copy only resulted in a quantitative reduction of shattering that might have been selected for in some but not all regions. Whether the 60 kb deletion including <i>DeSh1-9A</i> represents standing genetic variation or arose after fonio domestication cannot be determined. The deletion was not identified in any of the 14 re-sequenced <i>D. longiflora</i> accessions. Targeting the <i>DeSh1-9B</i> locus on chromosome 9B in accessions that carry the <i>DeSh1-9A</i> deletion through mutagenesis or genome editing could produce a fonio cultivar with significantly reduced seed shattering, which would form a first important step towards a significant improvement of this crop.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Plant material</h3><p>The plant material used in this study comprised a collection of 641 <i>D. exilis</i> accessions maintained at the French National Research Institute for Development (IRD), Montpellier, France<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Clément, J. &amp; Leblanc, J. M. Collecte IBPGR-ORSTOM de 1977 au Togo (Catalogue ORSTOM, 1984)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2504">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Barnaud, A. et al. Development of nuclear microsatellite markers for the fonio, Digitaria exilis (Poaceae), an understudied West African cereal. Am. J. Bot. 99, E105–E107 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2507">49</a></sup>. The collection was established from 1977 to 1988. For genome re-sequencing, a panel of 166 accessions (Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM10">6</a>) was selected based on geographic and bioclimatic data. One plant per accession was chosen for DNA extraction and sequencing. Inflorescences of sequenced <i>D. exilis</i> plants were covered with bags to prevent outcrossing. Grains of sequenced plants were collected and kept for further analyses. In addition, 17 accessions of <i>D. longiflora</i> were collected from specimens stored at the National Museum of Natural History of Paris (Paris, France), IFAN Ch. A. Diop (Dakar, Senegal), National Herbarium of The Netherlands (Leiden, Netherlands), and Cirad (Montpellier, France) (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Flow cytometry for genome size estimation</h3><p>The amount of nuclear DNA in fonio was measured by flow-cytometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Doležel, J., Greilhuber, J. &amp; Suda, J. Estimation of nuclear DNA content in plants using flow cytometry. Nat. Protoc. 2, 2233–2244 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2532">50</a></sup>. In brief, six different individual plants representing accession CM05836 were measured three times on three different days using a CyFlow® Space flow cytometer (Sysmex Partec GmbH, Görlitz, Germany) equipped with a 532 nm green laser. Soybean (<i>Glycine max</i> cultivar Polanka; 2C = 2.5 pg) was used as an internal reference standard. The gain of the instrument was adjusted so that the peak representing G1 nuclei of the standard was positioned approximately on channel 100 on a histogram of relative fluorescence intensity when using a 512-channel scale. The low level threshold was set to channel 20 to eliminate particles with the lowest fluorescent intensity from the histogram. All remaining fluorescent events were recorded with no further gating used. 2C DNA contents (in pg) were calculated from the mean G1 peak (interphase nuclei in G1 phase) positions by applying the formula:</p><div id="Equ1" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{2C}}\,{\mathrm{nuclear}}\,{\mathrm{DNA}}\,{\mathrm{content}} = \frac{{\left( {{\mathrm{sample}}\,{\mathrm{G1}}\,{\mathrm{peak}}\,{\mathrm{mean}}} \right) \times \left( {{\mathrm{standard}}\,{\mathrm{2C}}\,{\mathrm{DNA}}\,{\mathrm{content}}} \right)}}{{\left( {{\mathrm{standard}}\,{\mathrm{G1}}\,{\mathrm{peak}}\,{\mathrm{mean}}} \right)}}.$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (1)
                </div></div><p>DNA contents in pg were converted to genome sizes in bp using the conversion factor 1 pg DNA = 0.978 Gb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Doležel, J., Bartoš, J., Voglmayr, H. &amp; Greilhuber, J. Nuclear DNA content and genome size of trout and human. Cytometry 51A, 127–128 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2664">51</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Reference genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>High molecular weight (HMW) genomic DNA was isolated from a single CM05836 plant with a dark-treatment of 48 h before harvesting tissue from young leaves. Two paired-end and three mate-pair libraries were constructed with different insert sizes ranging from 450 bp to 10 kb. The 450 bp paired-end library was sequenced on an Illumina Hi-Seq 2500 instrument. The other libraries were sequenced on a Illumina NovaSeq 6000 instrument. In addition, one 10× linked read library was constructed and sequenced with an Illumina NovaSeq 6000 instrument, yielding 75-fold coverage. A de novo whole genome assembly (WGA) was performed with the DeNovoMAGIC3 software (NRGene).</p><p>For the super-scaffolding, one Hi-C library was generated using the Dovetail™ Hi-C Library Preparation Kit and sequenced on one lane of an Illumina HiSeq 4000 instrument, yielding ~128-fold coverage. The assembly to Hi-C super-scaffolds was performed with the HiRise<sup>TM</sup> software v2.0. This generated 18 large super-scaffolds with a size between 25.5 and 49.1 Mb (total size = 642.8 Mb with N50 = 39.9 Mb), while the 19th longest super-scaffold was only 1.5 Mb in size, indicating that the 18 longest super-scaffolds correspond to the 18 <i>D. exilis</i> chromosomes.</p><p>To generate a BioNano optical map, ultra HMW DNA was isolated using the QIAGEN Genomic-tip 500/G kit (Cat no./ID: 10262) from plants grown in a greenhouse with a dark treatment applied during 48 h prior to collecting leaf tissue. Labeling and staining of the HMW DNA were performed according to the Bionano Prep Direct Label and Stain (DLS) protocol (30206—Bionano Genomics) and then loaded on one Saphyr chip. The optical map was generated using the Bionano Genomics Saphyr System according the Saphyr System User Guide (30247—Bionano Genomics). A total of 1114.6 Gb of data were generated, but only 210.6 Gb of data corresponding to molecules with a size larger than 150 kb were retained. Hybrid scaffolding between the WGA and the optical maps was done with the hybridScaffold pipeline (with the Bionano Access v1.4 default parameters). In total, 39 hybrid scaffolds ranging from 550 kb to 40.9 Mb (total length 657.3 Mb with N50 = 22.6 Mb) were obtained (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><p>To construct the final chromosome-scale assembly, we integrated the Hi-C super-scaffolds and the hybrid scaffold manually. The 39 hybrid scaffolds generated with the optical map were aligned to the 18 Hi-C super-scaffolds produced by HiRise (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">9</a>). The hybrid scaffolds detected two chimeric DeNovoMAGIC3 scafflods that most likely arose from wrong connections in the putative centromeres. The final chromosome-level assembly was constructed as follows: (i) We used the hybrid assembly to break the two chimeric DeNovoMAGIC3 scaffolds, (ii) merged and oriented the hybrid scaffolds using the Hi-C super scaffolds, and (iii) merged the remaining contigs and scaffolds into an unanchored chromosome.</p><p>A second genome assembly was produced using the TRITEX pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Monat, C. et al. TRITEX: chromosome-scale sequence assembly of Triticeae genomes with open-source tools. Genome Biol. 20, 284 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2700">27</a></sup>. Briefly, the initial step using Illumina sequencing data (pre-processing of paired-end and mate-pair reads, unitig assembly, scaffolding and gap closing) was done according the instructions of the TRITEX pipeline [<a href="https://tritexassembly.bitbucket.io/">https://tritexassembly.bitbucket.io/</a>]. Integration of 10× and Hi-C reads was done differently. For the 10× scaffolding, we used tigmint v1.1.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Jackman, S. D. et al. Tigmint: correcting assembly errors using linked reads from large molecules. BMC Bioinforma. 19, 393 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2711">52</a></sup> to detect and cut sequences at positions with few spanning molecules, arks v1.0.3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Coombe, L. et al. ARKS: chromosome-scale scaffolding of human genome drafts with linked read kmers. BMC Bioinforma. 19, 234 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2715">53</a></sup> to generate graphs of scaffolds with connection evidence, and LINKS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Warren, R. L. et al. LINKS: scalable, alignment-free scaffolding of draft genomes with long reads. GigaScience 4, 35 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2719">54</a></sup> for a second step of scaffolding. For the Hi-C data, we used BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 26, 589–595 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2724">55</a></sup> to map the reads against the previous scaffolds and juicer tools v1.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Dudchenko, O. et al. De novo assembly of the Aedes aegypti genome using Hi-C yields chromosome-length scaffolds. Science 356, 92–95 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2728">56</a></sup> for the super-scaffolding.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Preparation of FISH probes and cytogenetic analyses</h3><p>Libraries of 45 bp long oligomers specific for each fonio pseudomolecule were designed using the Chorus software v1.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Han, Y. H., Zhang, T., Thammapichai, P., Weng, Y. Q. &amp; Jiang, J. M. Chromosome-specific painting in Cucumis species using bulked oligonucleotides. Genetics 200, 771–779 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2740">26</a></sup> [<a href="https://github.com/forrestzhang/Chorus">https://github.com/forrestzhang/Chorus</a>] with the following criteria: -p CGTGGTCGCGTCTCA -l 45 –homology 75 -d 10. The number of oligomers per pseudomolecule was adjusted to ensure uniform fluorescent signals along the entire chromosomes after fluorescence in situ hybridization (FISH). In total, 310,484 oligomers were selected (between 12,647 and 20,000 oligomers per library) and were synthesized by Arbor Biosciences (Ann Arbor, MI, USA). To prepare chromosome painting probes for FISH, the oligomer libraries were labeled directly using 6-FAM or CY3, or indirectly using digoxigenin or biotin. A tandem repeat CL10 with a 314 bp long repetitive unit (155 bp long subunit) was identified after the analysis of fonio DNA repeats with the RepeatExplorer software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Novak, P., Neumann, P., Pech, J., Steinhaisl, J. &amp; Macas, J. RepeatExplorer: a Galaxy-based web server for genome-wide characterization of eukaryotic repetitive elements from next-generation sequence reads. Bioinformatics 29, 792–793 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2751">57</a></sup>. A 20 bp oligomer was designed based on the CL10 sequence using the Primer3 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Untergasser, A. et al. Primer3–new capabilities and interfaces. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e115 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2755">58</a></sup> labeled by CY3 and used for FISH.</p><p>Chromosome spreads were prepared from actively growing roots (~1 cm long)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Šimoníková, D. et al. Chromosome painting facilitates anchoring reference genome sequence to chromosomes in situ and integrated karyotyping in banana (Musa Spp.). Front. Plant Sci. 10, 1503 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2762">59</a></sup>. Roots were collected into 50 mM phosphate buffer (pH 7.0) containing 0.2% β-mercaptoethanol, pre-treated in 0.05% 8-hydroxyquinoline for three hours at room temperature, fixed in 3:1 ethanol:acetic acid fixative overnight and stored in 70% ethanol at −20 °C. Chromosome spreads prepared from ~20 roots were washed in distilled water, 1× KCl buffer (pH 4) and root tips were incubated in enzyme mixture containing 4% cellulase and 2% macerozyme for 56 min at 37 °C. The reaction was stopped with TE buffer (pH 7.6), root tips were washed in 100% ethanol, then 30 µl of 9:1 ice-cold acetic acid:methanol mixture was added and root tips were broken with tweezers and dropped onto slide placed in a humid box. The preparations were air-dried, postfixed in 4% (v/v) formaldehyde solution in 2× SSC solution and used for fluorescence in situ hybridization (FISH). FISH was performed with the chromosome painting probes and a probe for the CL10 tandem repeat using a hybridization mixture (30 µl) containing 50% formamide, 10% dextran sulfate in 2× SSC and 10 ng/µl of labeled probe. Hybridization was carried out overnight at 37 °C. Digoxigenin-labeled and biotin-labeled probes were detected using anti-digoxigenin-FITC (Roche Applied Science) and streptavidin-Cy3 (ThermoFisher Scientific/Invitrogen), respectively. The preparations were mounted in Vectashield with DAPI (Vector laboratories, Ltd., Peterborough, UK) to counterstain the chromosomes and the slides were examined with an Axio Imager Z.2 Zeiss microscope (Zeiss, Oberkochen, Germany) equipped with a Cool Cube 1 camera (Metasystems, Altlussheim, Germany) and appropriate optical filters. The capture of fluorescence signals and measure of chromosome lengths were done using the ISIS software 5.4.7 (Metasystems) and final image adjustment was done in Adobe Photoshop CS5.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Analysis of fl-LTR-RT</h3><p>fl-LTR-RT were identified using both LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinforma. 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2775">60</a></sup> (-minlenltr 100 -maxlenltr 40000 -mintsd 4 -maxtsd 6 -motif TGCA -motifmis 1 -similar 85 -vic 10 -seed 20 -seqids yes) and LTR_finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2779">61</a></sup> (-D 40000 -d 100 -L 9000 -l 50 -p 20 -C -M 0.9). Then, the candidate LTRs were filtered using LTR_retriever<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Ou, S. J. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 1410–1422 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2783">62</a></sup>.</p><p>fl-LTRs were classified into families using a clustering approach using MeShClust2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="James, B. T., Luczak, B. B. &amp; Girgis, H. Z. MeShClust: an intelligent tool for clustering DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 46, e83 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2790">63</a></sup> with default parameters and manually curated with dotter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Sonnhammer, E. L. &amp; Durbin, R. A dot-matrix program with dynamic threshold control suited for genomic DNA and protein sequence analysis. Gene 167, GC1–GC10 (1995)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2794">64</a></sup> to discard wrongly assigned LTRs. Only LTR-RT families with at least 30 intact copies were considered in genetics analyses, where different families represented ‘populations’ and each fl-LTR-RT copy of a family was considered as an individual. A multiple sequence alignment was performed within each family using Clustal Omega<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Sievers, F. et al. Fast, scalable generation of high-quality protein multiple sequence alignments using Clustal Omega. Mol. Syst. Biol. 7, 539 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2798">65</a></sup> and the polymorphic sites were identified and converted into variant call format (VCF) using msa2vcf.jar [<a href="https://github.com/lindenb/jvarkit">https://github.com/lindenb/jvarkit</a>]. A principal component analysis (PCA) was performed for each fl-LTR-RT family using the R packages vcfR v.1.8.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Knaus, B. J. &amp; Grunwald, N. J. VCFR: a package to manipulate and visualize variant call format data in R. Mol. Ecol. Resour. 17, 44–53 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2809">66</a></sup> and adegenet v2.1.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Jombart, T. adegenet: a R package for the multivariate analysis of genetic markers. Bioinformatics 24, 1403–1405 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2814">67</a></sup>. Results were visualized with ggplot2 v3.3.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Wickham, H. ggplot2 - Elegant Graphics for Data Analysis (Springer International Publishing, 2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2818">68</a></sup>.</p><p>The approximate insertion dates of the fl-LTR-RTs were calculated using the evolutionary distance between two LTR sequences with the formula <i>T</i> = <i>K</i>/2<i>μ</i>, where <i>K</i> is the divergence rate approximated by percent identity and <i>μ</i> is the neutral mutation rate. The mutation rate used was <i>μ</i> = 1.3 × 10<sup>−8</sup> mutations per bp per year<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Ma, J. X. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404–12410 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2846">69</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Gene annotation</h3><p>A combination of homology-based and de novo approaches was used for repeat annotation using the RepeatModeler software [<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/</a>] and the RepeatExplorer software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Novak, P., Neumann, P., Pech, J., Steinhaisl, J. &amp; Macas, J. RepeatExplorer: a Galaxy-based web server for genome-wide characterization of eukaryotic repetitive elements from next-generation sequence reads. Bioinformatics 29, 792–793 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2865">57</a></sup>. The results of RepeatModeler, RepeatExplorer and LTR_retriever were merged into a comprehensive de novo repeat library using USEARCH v.11<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Edgar, R. C. Search and clustering orders of magnitude faster than BLAST. Bioinformatics 26, 2460–2461 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2869">70</a></sup> with default parameters (-id 0.9). The final repeats were classified using the RepeatClassifier module with the NCBI engine and then used to mask the CM05836 assembly.</p><p>The protein coding genes were predicted on the masked genome using MAKER (v3.01.02)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2876">71</a></sup>. Transcriptome reads from flag leaf, grain, panicle and above-ground seedlings were filtered for ribosomal RNA using SortMeRNA v2.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Kopylova, E., Noe, L. &amp; Touzet, H. SortMeRNA: fast and accurate filtering of ribosomal RNAs in metatranscriptomic data. Bioinformatics 28, 3211–3217 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2880">72</a></sup> and for adaptor sequences, quality and length using trimmomatic v0.38<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2884">73</a></sup>. Filtered reads were mapped to the reference sequence using STAR v.2.7.0d<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Dobin, A. et al. STAR: ultrafast universal RNA-seq aligner. Bioinformatics 29, 15–21 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2888">74</a></sup> and the alignments were assembled with StringTie v.1.3.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290–295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2892">75</a></sup> to further be used as transcript evidence. Protein sequences from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2899">76</a></sup>, <i>S. italica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Bennetzen, J. L. et al. Reference genome sequence of the model plant Setaria. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 555–561 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2905">28</a></sup>, <i>S. bicolor</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2911">77</a></sup>, <i>Z. mays</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2917">78</a></sup> and <i>O. sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2924">79</a></sup> were compared with BLASTX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389–3402 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2928">80</a></sup> to the masked pseudomolecules of CM05836 and alignments were filtered with Exonerate v.2.2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Slater, G. S. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinforma. 6, 31 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2932">81</a></sup> to search for the accurately spliced alignments. A de novo prediction was performed using ab initio softwares GeneMark-ES v.3.54<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Borodovsky, M. &amp; Lomsadze, A. Eukaryotic gene prediction using GeneMark.hmm-E and GeneMark-ES. Curr. Protoc. Bioinforma. 35, 4.6.1–4.6.10 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2936">82</a></sup>, SNAP v.2006-07-28<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinforma. 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2940">83</a></sup>, and Augustus v.2.5.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19, ii215–ii225 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2945">84</a></sup>. Three successive iterations of the pipeline MAKER were performed in order to improve the inference of the gene models and to integrate the final consensus genes. Finally, the putative gene functions were assigned using the UniProt/SwissProt database (Release 2019_08 – [<a href="https://www.uniprot.org/">https://www.uniprot.org/</a>]).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Synteny and comparative genome analysis</h3><p>To identify syntenic relationships, BLASTP (<i>E</i>-value 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>) was first used to perform all-versus-all protein comparisons between CM05836 and <i>S. italica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Bennetzen, J. L. et al. Reference genome sequence of the model plant Setaria. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 555–561 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2971">28</a></sup>, <i>Panicum miliaceum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Shi, J. P. et al. Chromosome conformation capture resolved near complete genome assembly of broomcorn millet. Nat. Commun. 10, 464 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2977">35</a></sup>, <i>Panicum hallii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Lovell, J. T. et al. The genomic landscape of molecular responses to natural drought stress in Panicum hallii. Nat. Commun. 9, 5213 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2984">85</a></sup>, <i>S. bicolor</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2990">77</a></sup>, <i>Z. mays</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d56235e2996">78</a></sup>, <i>O. sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3002">79</a></sup>, <i>B. distachyon</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3009">86</a></sup>, <i>H. vulgare</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427–433 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3015">87</a></sup>, <i>T. aestivum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding through a fully annotated and anchored reference genome sequence. Science 361, eaar7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3021">31</a></sup> and <i>A. tauschii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Genome sequence of the progenitor of the wheat D genome Aegilops tauschii. Nature 551, 498–502 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3027">88</a></sup>. Then, MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Wang, Y. P. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3032">89</a></sup> (-m 25; -s 10) was used for pairwise synteny block detection. For all the comparisons, fonio sub-genomes A and B were analyzed independently and only 1:1 relationships were retained (1:2 relationships for tetraploid broomcorn millet and the ancient tetraploid maize, where individual sub-genomes are not clearly phased).</p><p>To estimate the divergence time, we calculated the synonymous substitution rates (<i>K</i>s) for each homoeologous and orthologous pair. Briefly, nucleotide sequences were aligned with clustalW<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Thompson, J. D., Higgins, D. G. &amp; Gibson, T. J. Clustal-W - Improving the sensitivity of progressive multiple sequence alignment through sequence weighting, position-specific gap penalties and weight matrix choice. Nucleic Acids Res. 22, 4673–4680 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3042">90</a></sup> and the <i>K</i>s were calculated using the CODEML program in PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Yang, Z. H. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3049">91</a></sup>. Then, time of divergence events was calculated using <i>T</i> = <i>K</i>s/2<i>λ</i>, based on the clock-(<i>λ</i>) estimated for grasses of 6.5 × 10<sup>−9</sup> <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Gaut, B. S., Morton, B. R., McCaig, B. C. &amp; Clegg, M. T. Substitution rate comparisons between grasses and palms: synonymous rate differences at the nuclear gene Adh parallel rate differences at the plastid gene rbcL. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 93, 10274–10279 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3068">92</a></sup>. We used the maximum likelihood for gene-order analysis (MLGO) tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Hu, F., Lin, Y. &amp; Tang, J. MLGO: phylogeny reconstruction and ancestral inference from gene-order data. BMC Bioinforma. 15, 354 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3072">93</a></sup> to reconstruct the hypothetical ancestral genome of the Paniceae tribe from syntenic‐block data of <i>D. exilis</i>, <i>S. italica</i>, <i>P. miliaceum</i>, and <i>P. hallii</i>. Syntenic blocks were identified using MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Wang, Y. P. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3089">89</a></sup> using the B sub-genome of <i>D. exilis</i> as reference. Then, individual syntenic blocks across all selected species were analyzed according their physical position and orientation in order to combine them into syntenic-block markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Ren, L., Huang, W. &amp; Cannon, S. B. Reconstruction of ancestral genome reveals chromosome evolution history for selected legume species. New Phytol. 223, 2090–2103 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3097">94</a></sup> (Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM6">2</a>). Syntenic-block markers along with the known species tree were provided as inputs to the MLGO server to infer the ancestral state of the Paniceae.</p><p>To analyze genome dominance, the quantification of transcript abundance and expression were calculated using RSEM v1.3.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Li, B. &amp; Dewey, C. N. RSEM: accurate transcript quantification from RNA-Seq data with or without a reference genome. BMC Bioinforma. 12, 323 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3107">95</a></sup> and genes were considered as expressed when expression was &gt;0.5 TPM in at least one tissue. To study the homoeolog expression bias a two-sided Mann–Whitney <i>U</i> test was used to examine if the TPM values of sub-genomes A and B differed significantly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Re-sequencing of <i>D. exilis</i> and <i>D. longiflora</i> accessions</h3><p>One or two young leaves from <i>D. exilis</i> or <i>D. longiflora</i> seedlings were collected in 2 ml Eppendorf tubes, flash-frozen in liquid nitrogen, and ground with glass beads in a SPEX SamplePrep Geno/Grinder 2010. Samples were mixed with 1.2 ml 2× CTAB buffer (2% CTAB, 200 mM Tris/HCl (pH 8), 20 mM EDTA, 1.4 M NaCl, 1.0% PVP, 0.28 M β-mercaptoethanol) and incubated at 65 °C with periodic mixing for 60 min. Samples were cooled to room temperature and centrifuged at 2000 × <i>g</i> for 10 min. Subsequently, 900 μl of the supernatant was transferred to a fresh 2 ml Eppendorf tube and incubated with 800 μl dichloromethane:isoamyl alcohol (24:1) in an overhead-shaker at half-speed at 4 °C for 15 min. After centrifugation at 10,000 × <i>g</i> for 15 min, 800 μl of the supernatant was transferred to a fresh 2 ml Eppendorf tube and incubated with 5 μl RNase A (10 mg/ml, EN0531, ThermoFisher Scientific) at 37 °C for 15 min. DNA was precipitated by adding 560 μl isopropanol and mixing the tubes by inversion. Precipitated DNA was pelleted by centrifugation at 4 °C and 10,000 × <i>g</i> for 10 min and the supernatant discarded. The DNA pellet was washed in a first ethanol wash (76% ethanol, 200 mM sodium acetate) for 15 min and in a second ethanol wash (76% ethanol, 10 mM ammonium acetate) for 5 min. Subsequently, DNA pellets were air-dried to remove residual ethanol and eluted in 50 μl TE buffer (10 mM Tris/HCl (pH 8), 1 mM EDTA). Extracted DNA was quantified with the Qubit dsDNA HS Assay (Q32851, ThermoFisher Scientific), the purity was confirmed by checking 260/280 and 260/230 ratios on a Nanodrop spectrophotometer, and the integrity was confirmed by analyzing 1 μl per sample on a 1% TAE agarose gel. Library preparation and sequencing were performed by Novogene. Briefly, 1.0 μg DNA per sample was used as input material for the DNA sample preparations. Sequencing libraries were generated using the NEBNext® Ultra II DNA Library Prep Kit following manufacturer’s instructions. Libraries were sequenced using an Illumina NovaSeq 6000 system.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Mapping of re-sequencing data and variant calling</h3><p>For quality control of each sample, raw sequence reads were analyzed with the fastqQC tool-v0.11.7 and low-quality reads were filtered with trimmomatic-v0.38<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3152">73</a></sup> using the following criteria: LEADING:20; TRAILING:20; SLIDINGWINDOW:5:20 and MINLEN:50. The filtered paired-end reads were then aligned for each sample individually against the CM05836 reference assembly using BWA-MEM (v0.7.17-r1188)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 26, 589–595 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3156">55</a></sup> followed by sorting and indexing using samtools (v1.6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3160">96</a></sup>. Alignment summary, base quality score and insert size metrics were collected and duplicated reads were marked and read groups were assigned using the Picard tools [<a href="http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/">http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/</a>]. Variants were identified with GATK&nbsp;v3.8<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3171">97</a></sup> using the emitRefConfidence function of the HaplotypeCaller algorithm to call SNPs and InDels for each accession followed by a joint genotyping step performed by GenotypeGVCFs. To obtain high confidence variants, we excluded SNPs and InDels with the VariantFiltration function of GATK with the criteria: QD &lt; 2.0; FS &gt; 60.0; MQ &lt; 40.0; MQRankSum &lt; −12.5; ReadPosRankSum &lt; −8.0 and SOR &gt; 4.0. The complete automated pipeline has been compiled and is available on github [<a href="https://github.com/IBEXCluster/IBEX-SNPcaller">https://github.com/IBEXCluster/IBEX-SNPcaller</a>].</p><p>A total of 36.5 million variants were called. Raw variants were filtered using GATK v3.8<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3186">97</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Van der Auwera, G. A. et al. From FastQ data to high confidence variant calls: the Genome Analysis Toolkit best practices pipeline. Curr. Protoc. Bioinforma. 43, 11 10 1-11 10 33 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3189">98</a></sup> and VCFtools v0.1.17<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156–2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3193">99</a></sup>. Variants located on chromosome unanchored, InDels, ‘SNP clusters’ defined as three or more SNPs located within 10 bp, missing data &gt;10%, low and high average SNP depth (14 ≤ DP ≥ 42), and accessions having more than 33% of missing data were discarded. Only biallelic SNPs were retained to perform further analyses representing a final VCF file of 11,046,501 SNPs (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM1">7</a>). These variants were annotated using snpEff v4.3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Cingolani, P. et al. A program for annotating and predicting the effects of single nucleotide polymorphisms, SnpEff: SNPs in the genome of Drosophila melanogaster strain w(1118); iso-2; iso-3. Fly 6, 80–92 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3200">100</a></sup> with the CM05836 gene models.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genetic diversity and population structure</h3><p>Genetic diversity and population structure analyses were performed using <i>D. exilis</i> and <i>D. longiflora</i> accessions together, or with <i>D. exilis</i> accessions alone. PCA and individual ancestry coefficients estimation were performed using the R package LEA v2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Frichot, E. &amp; Francois, O. LEA: an R package for landscape and ecological association studies. Methods Ecol. Evol. 6, 925–929 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3221">101</a></sup>. The geographical projection of the first PCA axis of the <i>D. exilis</i> samples was done and visualized using the Kriging function in the fields v10.3 R package&nbsp;[<a href="https://cran.r-project.org/web/packages/fields">https://cran.r-project.org/web/packages/fields</a>]<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Nychka, D., Furrer, R., Paige, J. &amp; Sain, S. fields: Tools for Spatial Data. Retrieved from 
https://cran.r-project.org/package=fields

 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3236">102</a></sup>. For ancestry coefficients analyses, the snmf function was used with 10 independent runs for each <i>K</i> from <i>K</i> = 2 to <i>K</i> = 10. The optimal <i>K</i>, indicating the most likely number of ancestral populations, was determined with the cross-validation error rate. For the two analyses, we considered only SNPs present in at least two accessions (i.e., private SNPs were excluded). We also studied the geographic distribution of private SNPs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Cubry, P., Vigouroux, Y. &amp; Francois, O. The empirical distribution of singletons for geographic samples of DNA sequences. Front. Genet. 8, 139 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3253">103</a></sup> (i.e., SNP present only once in a single accession). Genome-wide pairwise LD was estimated independently for <i>D. exilis</i> (2,617,322 SNPs) and <i>D. longiflora</i> (9,839,152 SNPs). LD decay (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup>) was calculated using the tool PopLDdecay v3.40<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Zhang, C., Dong, S. S., Xu, J. Y., He, W. M. &amp; Yang, T. L. PopLDdecay: a fast and effective tool for linkage disequilibrium decay analysis based on variant call format files. Bioinformatics 35, 1786–1788 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3267">104</a></sup> in a 500 kb distance and plotted using the ggplot2 v3.3.2 package in R<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Wickham, H. ggplot2 - Elegant Graphics for Data Analysis (Springer International Publishing, 2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3272">68</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Association of climate, geography, and social factors with the population genetics structure</h3><p>Natural (i.e., climate, geography) and human (i.e., ethnic and linguistic) factors were tested for an association with the genetic structure of <i>D. exilis</i> accessions. Bioclimate data and ethnic and linguistic data were extracted for each of the 166 re-sequenced <i>D. exilis</i> accessions from WorldClim v1.4 [<a href="https://www.worldclim.org/data/v1.4/worldclim14.html">https://www.worldclim.org/data/v1.4/worldclim14.html</a>]<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Hijmans, R. J., Cameron, S. E., Parra, J. L., Jones, P. G. &amp; Jarvis, A. Very high resolution interpolated climate surfaces for global land areas. Int. J. Climatol. 25, 1965–1978 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3298">105</a></sup>, passport data (ethnic groups), and Ethnologue v16 (language, [<a href="https://www.ethnologue.com/">https://www.ethnologue.com/</a>]). The associations between the genetic structure and different factors were evaluated statistically using Pearson’s correlation coefficient test (two-sided) for the bioclimatic and geographic data with the R package stats v3.6.0 (function cor). For the associations of social factors, two-sided Mantel tests (ecodist R package v2.0.5 [<a href="https://rdrr.io/cran/ecodist/">https://rdrr.io/cran/ecodist/</a>]) with 999 permutations were performed between the genetic distance matrix of <i>D. exilis</i> and the dissimilarity matrix of social factors as inputs. Fisher’s exact tests (stats R package v3.6.0) were performed with the structure data at <i>K</i> = 6 and accessions having ancestry thresholds of &gt;70% as input. The <i>p</i>-values were computed by Monte Carlo simulation. We used analysis of covariance (ANCOVA) to test for the impact of ethnolinguistic factors on the first PCA coordinates while controlling for other covariates: climatic and geographic variables.</p><p>Synthetic climate variables were retrieved from the WorldClim v1.4 database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Hijmans, R. J., Cameron, S. E., Parra, J. L., Jones, P. G. &amp; Jarvis, A. Very high resolution interpolated climate surfaces for global land areas. Int. J. Climatol. 25, 1965–1978 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3329">105</a></sup>. We focused our analysis on two climate variables that were the most pertinent to annual species, i.e., mean temperature of the wettest quarter (bio8) and mean precipitation of the wettest quarter (bio 16), respectively. A GWAS with these climate variables has been performed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Cubry, P. et al. Genome wide association study pinpoints key agronomic QTLs in African rice Oryza glaberrima. Preprint at 
https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.01.07.897298

 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3333">106</a></sup>. Briefly, we first used the impute method from the R package LEA v2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Frichot, E. &amp; Francois, O. LEA: an R package for landscape and ecological association studies. Methods Ecol. Evol. 6, 925–929 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3337">101</a></sup> to fill the gaps in the genotypic matrix in order to achieve greater power. We then filtered out SNPs with a MAF below 5%. We used three different models of association to perform the GWAS, efficient mixed-model association (EMMA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Kang, H. M. et al. Efficient control of population structure in model organism association mapping. Genetics 178, 1709–1723 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3341">107</a></sup> using the R package emma v1.1.2, mixed linear model (MLM) as implemented in the R package gapit v3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 108" title="Lipka, A. E. et al. GAPIT: genome association and prediction integrated tool. Bioinformatics 28, 2397–2399 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR108" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3345">108</a></sup> and latent factor mixed models (LFMM) using LFMM2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 109" title="Caye, K., Jumentier, B., Lepeule, J. &amp; Francois, O. LFMM 2: fast and accurate inference of gene-environment associations in genome-wide studies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 36, 852–860 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR109" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3350">109</a></sup> as implemented in lfmm R package v2.0. For gapit (MLM), we considered six principal components, which we chose from the previously described structure analysis. The LFMM v2 method implements a Latent Factor Mixed Model that estimates unknown confounding factors (the latent factors) jointly using the genotypic and phenotypic matrices. For this specific model, the estimation of latent factors was performed using both the phenotypic data (i.e., the climate variable in consideration) and a genotypic matrix of SNPs with a MAF higher than 20%. Once the GWAS step was performed, we used a FDR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 110" title="Benjamini, Y. &amp; Hochberg, Y. Controlling the false discovery rate: a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J. R. Stat. Soc. Ser. (Methdol.) 57, 289–300 (1995)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR110" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3354">110</a></sup> approach to estimate the significance of the associations for each analysis, retaining a FDR threshold of 5%, resulting in a list of candidate positions for each variable and each GWAS method. We then considered a genomic window of 50 kb upstream and 50 kb downstream for each position as a candidate region, which we denominated quantitative trait locus (QTL). When two QTLs overlapped, they were combined.</p><p>To test SNP associations to ethnolinguistic groups, we used the PLINK software v1.90<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="Purcell, S. et al. PLINK: A tool set for whole-genome association and population-based linkage analyses. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 81, 559–575 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3361">111</a></sup>. We filtered out the SNPs with MAF below 5%, more than 10% missing data, and in LD, resulting in 897,796 SNPs. The association of SNPs with each ethnic and linguistic group was tested by a logistic regression model assuming additive genetic effects, using three principal components as covariates to control for population stratification. Significant associations were identified above the threshold of <i>p</i> value = 1e<sup>−05</sup>. Manhattan plots were plotted to display the associations using the qqman R package v0.1.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 112" title="Turner, S. D. qqman: an R package for visualizing GWAS results usingQ-Q and Manhattan plots. J. Open Source Softw. 3, 731 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR112" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3370">112</a></sup>. GO terms for all fonio protein sequences were assigned using the DeepGOPlus model [<a href="https://github.com/bio-ontology-research-group/deepgoplus">https://github.com/bio-ontology-research-group/deepgoplus</a>]<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 113" title="Kulmanov, M. &amp; Hoehndorf, R. DeepGOPlus: improved protein function prediction from sequence. Bioinformatics 36, 422–429 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR113" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3382">113</a></sup>. We only considered GO labels that have a confident score &gt;0.3. Enrichment analysis for GO biological processes, cellular components, and molecular functions of the genes of interests from climate association analyses was performed using the Fisher exact tests implemented in R package topGO v3.36.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 114" title="Alexa, A., Rahnenfuhrer, J. &amp; Lengauer, T. Improved scoring of functional groups from gene expression data by decorrelating GO graph structure. Bioinformatics 22, 1600–1607 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR114" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3386">114</a></sup> (algorithm = ‘weight01’). We reported GO terms with enrichment <i>p</i> value &lt; 0.05 for each GO-term category.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Demographic history reconstruction</h3><p>A Sequentially Markovian Coalescent-based approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 115" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Inference of human population history from individual whole-genome sequences. Nature 475, 493–496 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR115" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3401">115</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 116" title="Schiffels, S. &amp; Durbin, R. Inferring human population size and separation history from multiple genome sequences. Nat. Genet. 46, 919–925 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR116" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3404">116</a></sup> was used to infer past evolution of the effective population size of <i>D. exilis</i> and <i>D. longiflora</i>. This analysis was made with the smc++<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 117" title="Terhorst, J., Kamm, J. A. &amp; Song, Y. S. Robust and scalable inference of population history froth hundreds of unphased whole genomes. Nat. Genet. 49, 303–309 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR117" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3414">117</a></sup> software and additional customized scripts [<a href="https://github.com/Africrop/fonio_smcpp">https://github.com/Africrop/fonio_smcpp</a>]. We excluded SNPs that were not usable (e.g. repeated regions) according to msmc-tools advices [<a href="https://github.com/stschiff/msmc-tools">https://github.com/stschiff/msmc-tools</a>]. Analysis was done with all nine individuals from the genetically closest group of <i>D. longiflora</i> and also with the six groups of <i>D. exilis</i> based on the genetic structure. The smc++ vcf2smc tools was used to generate the input file and smc++ inference was performed for the different sets of genotypes using the smc++ cv command, considering 200 and 25,000 generations lower and upper time points boundaries, respectively. All other options were set to default. We considered a generation time of one year and a mutation rate of 6.5 × 10<sup>−9</sup>. Different sets of distinguished lineages were considered for the different sets of individuals (all the samples were considered as distinguished). Graphical representation was made using the ggplot2 v3.3.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Wickham, H. ggplot2 - Elegant Graphics for Data Analysis (Springer International Publishing, 2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3441">68</a></sup> package for R.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Genome scanning for selection signals</h3><p>Detection of selection was performed independently with three different methods: (1) Weir and Cockerham <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>; (2) nucleotide diversity ratios (<i>π</i>) were calculated between <i>D. exilis</i> and <i>D. longiflora</i> using VCFtools v0.1.17<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156–2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3466">99</a></sup> with sliding windows of 50 kb every 10 kb; (3) SweeD v3.3.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Pavlidis, P., Zivkovic, D., Stamatakis, A. &amp; Alachiotis, N. SweeD: Likelihood-based detection of selective sweeps in thousands of genomes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2224–2234 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3471">39</a></sup> was used on <i>D. exilis</i> to detect signatures of selective sweeps based on the composite likelihood ratio (CLR) test within non-overlapping intervals of 3000 bp along each chromosome. We grouped CLR peak positions into one common window if two or several peaks were &lt;100 kb apart. Candidate genomic regions were selected for each method based on top 1% values. A list comprising 34 well-characterized domestication genes (Supplementary Data&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM12">8</a>) from major crops was selected and compared by BLAST v2.6.0 against gene models and the genome assembly of <i>D. exilis</i> accession CM05836. Each putative orthologous gene was validated by local alignment using ClustalW v2.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Thompson, J. D., Higgins, D. G. &amp; Gibson, T. J. Clustal-W - Improving the sensitivity of progressive multiple sequence alignment through sequence weighting, position-specific gap penalties and weight matrix choice. Nucleic Acids Res. 22, 4673–4680 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3484">90</a></sup>. We considered the genes as orthologous when ≥ 70% of the coding sequence (CDS) length hit the genomic sequence of <i>D. exilis</i> with at least 70% identity. Validated orthologous genes were crossed with genomic regions under selection using bedtools v2.28.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 118" title="Quinlan, A. R. &amp; Hall, I. M. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics 26, 841–842 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR118" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3492">118</a></sup>. To assess the effect of ∆<i>DeSh1</i> on seeds shattering, fonio panicles with three racemes were collected from mature plants and placed in 15 ml tubes. Individual tubes were shaken using a Geno Grinder tissue homogenizer (SPEX SamplePrep, Metuchen, NJ) at 1350 rpm speed for 20 s and the percentage of shattering was calculated. ANOVA test was performed with the MVApp [<a href="https://mvapp.kaust.edu.sa/">https://mvapp.kaust.edu.sa/</a>]<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 119" title="Julkowska, M. M. et al. MVApp-Multivariate analysis application for streamlined data analysis and curation. Plant Physiol. 180, 1261–1276 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#ref-CR119" id="ref-link-section-d56235e3506">119</a></sup>. Two specific primer pairs were designed to confirm the 60 kb deletion. The first primer pair amplifies ̴1 kb fragment within the <i>DeSh1-9A</i> gene (forward primer 5′-GTGACCATGCCAAGCAGGCG-3′; reverse primer 5′-GCTAGCTTGAGGTATTTACGG-3′). The second primer pair was designed in the flanking region of the deletion and was used to amplify ~750 bp fragment in accessions that carry the deletion (forward primer 5′-GCCGTATATAGTTGCCGCATCA-3′; reverse primer 5′-CCTACCGTTAGATCCGTGCGGA-3′).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and its Supplementary Information files. A reporting summary for this Article is available as a Supplementary Information file. The datasets generated and analyzed during the current study are available from the corresponding author upon request. The raw sequencing data used for de novo whole-genome assembly, the raw bionano map, the CM05836 genome assembly, the RNA-seq data for the annotation and the 183 re-sequenced accessions of <i>D. exilis</i> and <i>D. longiflora</i> for the population genomics analysis are available on EBI-ENA under the study number PRJEB36539 [<a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/browser/view/PRJEB36539">https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/browser/view/PRJEB36539</a>]. The annotation of the CM05836 genome, the Tritex assembly, the probes for the chromosome painting experiment, the gene ontology annotation, the VCF file and <i>DeSh1</i> phenotyping data are available on the DRYAD database [<a href="https://doi.org/10.5061/dryad.2v6wwpzj0">https://doi.org/10.5061/dryad.2v6wwpzj0</a>]. The plant coding sequences [<a href="https://plants.ensembl.org">https://plants.ensembl.org</a>] and [<a href="https://genomevolution.org/coge/">https://genomevolution.org/coge/</a>], the UniProt/SwissProt database (Release 2019_08–[<a href="https://www.uniprot.org/">https://www.uniprot.org/</a>]), the embryophyta_odb9 BUSCO dataset [<a href="https://busco-archive.ezlab.org/v2/datasets/embryophyta_odb9.tar.gz">https://busco-archive.ezlab.org/v2/datasets/embryophyta_odb9.tar.gz</a>], the Ethnologue version 16 language [<a href="https://www.ethnologue.com/">https://www.ethnologue.com/</a>]), and WorldClim version 1.4 [<a href="https://www.worldclim.org/data/v1.4/worldclim14.html">https://www.worldclim.org/data/v1.4/worldclim14.html</a>] databases were downloaded from source for data analyses. Source data are provided with this paper.</p>
            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="code-availability" data-title="Code availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="code-availability-content">
              
              <p>Custom scripts for the SNP calling are available on GitHub: [<a href="https://github.com/IBEXCluster/IBEX-SNPcaller">https://github.com/IBEXCluster/IBEX-SNPcaller</a>]. Custom scripts used to launch the smc++ analysis for fonio genome resequencing data are available on GitHub: [<a href="https://github.com/Africrop/fonio_smcpp">https://github.com/Africrop/fonio_smcpp</a>].</p>

N.K. and M.A. established the SNP calling pipeline. J.B., M.C., and L.Z. performed molecular analyses and constructed sequencing libraries. M.A., H.I.A., P.C., L.G., N.S., and T.W. performed bioinformatics analyses. H.I.A, P.C. and Y.P. performed association studies. M.A., H.I.A., P.C., Y.V., C.B., A.B., and S.G.K. interpret results. D.S., J.C., E.H., and J.D. performed flow-cytometry and chromosome painting. S.A., S.C., and H.B. constructed and analyzed the CM05836 optical map. Y.P. and H.I.A carried out phenotypic data collection and analyses. J.J.W., M.G., N.A.K., C.L., S.C., S.V., and C.B. contributed biological materials. M.A., H.I.A., and S.G.K. wrote the paper with substantial inputs from P.C., Y.V., and A.B. All authors have read and approved the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to

                    <i>Nat Commun</i> <b>11, </b>4488 (2020). https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-020-18329-4</p><p class="c-bibliographic-information__download-citation u-hide-print"><a data-test="citation-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="download article citation" data-track-label="link" data-track-external="" href="/articles/s41467-020-18329-4.ris">Download citation<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-download"></use></svg></a></p><ul class="c-bibliographic-information__list" data-test="publication-history"><li class="c-bibliographic-information__list-item"><p>Received<span class="u-hide">: </span><span class="c-bibliographic-information__value"><time datetime="2020-04-13">13 April 2020</time></span></p></li><li class="c-bibliographic-information__list-item"><p>Accepted<span class="u-hide">: </span><span class="c-bibliographic-information__value"><time datetime="2020-08-16">16 August 2020</time></span></p></li><li class="c-bibliographic-information__list-item"><p>Published<span class="u-hide">: </span><span class="c-bibliographic-information__value"><time datetime="2020-09-08">08 September 2020</time></span></p></li><li class="c-bibliographic-information__list-item c-bibliographic-information__list-item--doi"><p><abbr title="Digital Object Identifier">DOI</abbr><span class="u-hide">: </span><span class="c-bibliographic-information__value"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-020-18329-4" data-track="click" data-track-action="view doi" data-track-label="link" itemprop="sameAs">https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-020-18329-4</a></span></p></li></ul><div data-component="share-box"></div><div data-component="article-info-list"></div></div></div></div></div></section>
